	panose-1:2 11 6 4 2 2 2 2 2 4;}

<div class="word-content">
<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:14.0pt'>GETTING THE POINT</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Virgil Warren, PhD</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>INTRODUCTION: The Nature of Language Communication</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  A.
An allegory: parallels between working a puzzle and interpreting the Bible</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  1. Not everything is all there
together; we have to put it together. The Bible has a historical organization
and a practical application to specific situations.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   2. We are tempted to start putting pieces
together before we get all the pieces turned over and sorted.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  3. We begin by turning the pieces
over and sorting them by color and perhaps shape, a procedure comparable to
getting the data together before beginning to work on a conclusion.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   4. In a puzzle each part has to fit
every other part; there is interconnection and wholeness. Biblical
interpretation goes beyond believing passages in theory without saying what
they mean.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  5. We do the most obvious parts
first: the edge pieces, a line like a horizon that goes through the picture</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  6. The process gets easier the
farther it goes; the picture gets clearer as more becomes visible.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  7. Sometimes we can force pieces in
where they do not belong. We may even whittle them a little!</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  8. Putting the wrong piece somewhere
keeps the rest from going together.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-1.0in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   9. Sometimes
we are surprised where a piece goesas where a color change occurs at the edge
of the piece. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   10. One important piece may connect
one group of pieces with another group of pieces that we have been working on.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   11. Some of us have a better eye for
working puzzles than others do.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   12. Sometimes pieces are missing.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> B. Nature
of language </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  1.
Language always comes from the subjective viewpoint</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:1.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-1.25in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a. <u>Literal language</u> exists
although language comes from the subjective side.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <i>Literal</i> means the
most common (often the most concrete) usage of a word or expression. Speakers
do not say everything about what they are describing; not all statements have
the character of a definition. A definition not only describes what something
is; it marks the <u>end</u> of itwhat it is not. <b>(1)</b> Speakers say only
enough to bring up the full awareness in the other persons mind. They do not say
it all because hearers can usually fill out much of the picture from their own
experience. Horseless carriage notes that there is no horse to carry it along;
that much is in the word picture. But the fact that it carries people rather
than freight is not expressed, nor that it has a cooling system and pistons. Of
course, if hearers do not know from experience the nature of what a speaker is referencing,
they will misunderstand or not understand; the words themselves do not
communicate a complete picture. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2)
Speakers speak from their own perception, which is not the same as the object
itself. Thought and language omit things and add things to what is actually
there. Speaking of part to bring all to the hearers mind goes even further in
figures of speech like metonymy or metaphor. <b>(3) </b>They speak only of the
part they want to communicate. The trust factor is that, although perception does
not have a one-for-one equivalency with the referent, they are close enough for
practical purposes. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   (a) There is a
language-concept distinction. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>   (b)
There is a language-referent distinction.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Meaning has three thrusts: [1] what the statement is
referring to, and [2] how that referent relates to the rest of the truth, and
[3] how the whole picture relates to the present situation.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>Luke <b>4</b>:3 illustrates the
subjective-objective distinction. Satan says, <i>If you are the son of God,
tell this stone to become bread. </i>Some commentators know that this is what
Greek grammarians call an A-class conditional sentence, a so-called real
condition. They take the implication to be <i>If you are Gods son </i>[and
you are]<i>, tell this stone to become bread. </i>Satan is not implying,
however, one way or the other whether Jesus is Gods Son. It is a hypothetical
statement in which the idea is viewed as true for the purposes of
communication, or for the sake of the argument. Real condition does not
mean it is <u>real</u>; it means the condition is <u>treated as real</u>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The
language-reality distinction creates the possibility of being wrong, using the
same word for different things, using the same term analogically to label
functional equivalents in another system, just plain lying. The
language-reality is shown by word-meaning changes, by different words for the
same thing, by different languages. Language takes place in the mind, not in
the reality; hence, we do not really ask What happened? but How did the
writer perceive what happened?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The
language-referent distinction applies to the <u>emphasis</u> a speaker makes in
a statement. The stress he puts on one item over another does not come from its
relative importance in something, but from the point of the conversation, from
the speakers perception and interest at the moment. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Similarly, grammatical
dependence-independence does not necessarily correspond to the relative
importance or centrality of something. For instance, make disciples is not
necessarily the most important element in the Great Commission because it is
the only verbal idea carried by a finite verbwhereas participles carry the other
three verbal ideas. That decision comes from the nature of the four elements in
the statement. For further material on this point (reification), see below
under Confusing Grammar and Reality.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b. <u>Figurative language</u>
is possible because language comes from the subjective side. Figures of speech
include metaphor, simile, synecdoche, metonymy, myth. Whereas literal
language stresses cognitive, scientific, objective matters, figurative language
<b>(1)</b> stresses <u>affective, subjective matters</u>; it creates impressions,
exhorts, enlivens. Language is least adequate in affective areas, and for that
reason figurative expression is used to take up some slack. Subjective things
like feelings are hardest to communicate because they are always subjective to
the speaker. Figurative language <b>(2) </b>communicates the <u>unknown and
unknowable</u>. It <b>(3)</b> makes <u>abstract matters concrete and tangible</u>.
<b>(4)</b> Figurative language achieves an <u>economy of expression</u> and, <b>(5)</b>
makes information more <u>memorable</u>. For more on figurative <i>vs</i>.
literal, see Interpretation of Classes of Literature.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c. <u>Phenomenological
language</u> also comes from the subjective side. Whereas figurative language
addresses the <u>affective</u> dimension, phenomenological language keys off of
the <u>appearance</u> of things. <i>A watched pot never boils</i> refers to
the way it seems, even though we know it is not true (real), strictly
(literally) speaking. It contrasts with scientific usage, which speaks as
much as possible in keeping with the objective nature of things. Phenomenological
language relates to the word picture in a term, its derivation, morphemes,
but especially its appearance.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>**Phenomenological language applies to
Old Testament passages that sound as if there is <u>no existence after death</u>
(Ecclesiastes. <b>3</b>:18-22).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  **The
notion of <u>touch</u> is phenomenological language because contact </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'> does
not really occur.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  **<u>Sucking</u>
speaks the way it looks. Vacuums do not draw; the absence of air allows other
air to push in.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  **<u>Sunrise</u>
is a common example of speaking from appearance. We say that the sun came up or
went down though we believe the earth is turning to create that other
impression.     **<u>Indwelling
of the Spirit</u> pictures as internal what is, in fact, inter-</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'> personal.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  **The
doctrine of <u>natural depravity</u> isat least in partdue to reading
phenomenological language as scientific expression. Psalm <b>51</b>:5 and <b>58</b>:3
could be considered cases of such language; that is certainly the ways people
seem.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  **Bowels
of compassion became an expression because extreme feelings give us a peculiar
sensation in the stomach: <i>That made me sick to my stomach.</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  **<i>I
never knew you </i>(Matthew <b>7</b>:23) may work like <i>A watched pot never
boils.</i> It could also be effective proleptic expression: the way things
turn out may as well be the way they always were.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  **Death is
pictured as sleep. (For references, see The Doctrine of Soul-</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'> Sleeping.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  d. <u>Poetic language</u><b> </b>shows
that language comes from the subjective side as another kind of figurative
language. Poetic language <u>pictures</u> with <u>exaggeration</u> for emphasis,
as in the case of the imprecatory psalms and the imprecatory apocalyptic
material in Revelation.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>  Poetic
language can talk about trees clapping their hands, a sacrifice of praise, God
inhabiting praise.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>New Testament language about eternal
suffering in fire and brimstone may instance cases of poetic language. Conditional
immortality, annihilationism, and universalism attempt to provide answers to the
specter of a loving God torturing someone for eternity. The need for those
solutions comes from reading poetic language as strict speech.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  2.
Words do not always mean the same thing.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <i>The relationship between
the forms of a language and the meanings they express is seldom one-to-one.</i>
The same word does not always mean the same thing; different words do not
necessarily mean different things; words that can substitute for each other are
not necessarily exact synonyms</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>A difference could be made between a
city <u>set</u> on a hill and a city <u>built</u> on a hill. <i>Set</i> and <i>built</i>
are not synonyms; yet their different word pictures can be used to communicate
the same idea at a general level. An automatic can be a type of gun or a kind
of car. Especially when an expression is peculiar to one writer, it is not
likely to be referring to something entirely absent elsewhere in scripture. Examples
include the filling terminology in Luke, the earnest of the Spirit
expression in Paul, or baptism in the Holy Spirit in the mouth of John the
Baptist (also in 1 Corinthians <b>12</b>:13).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> People sometimes fail to distinguish
word and meaningwhat it says and what it means. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>In a book <i>The Eldership</i>, J. W.
McGarvey confused language and reality when he argued that <i>husband of one
wife </i>means that an elder must be married because a one-woman man is not
a no-woman man. That a one-woman man is not a no-woman man makes an
observation about <u>reality</u>, but Pauls <u>statement</u> contrasts with
more than one without also contrasting with less than one. His frame of reference
is a character issue as Titus <b>1</b> shows: <i>. . . because a bishop must
be blameless.</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> People reason incorrectlyas
sophistswhen they make words always have the same sense. They combine
statements whose words are the same but with different referent and treat them
as if they mean the same thing, thereby drawing specious conclusions.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Speakers depend on the setting
to fine tune their terms (law of harmony, law of opposition, nature of the
case</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>).</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'> Tat
phenomenon is partly what is going on in regard to womens teaching men. Teaching
evidently does not mean teaching in the general sense of transferring
information, but teaching in the sense of normative teaching as done by an
elder. Teaching may be unofficial, as when the Pharisees say to the man born
blind, <i>You were altogether born in sin, and you teach us? </i>(John <b>9</b>:34).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Words may be used in <u>both general
and technical</u> senses</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#6600AC'> <i>Moron</i>,
<i>imbecile</i>, and <i>idiot</i> are interchangeable in common parlance, but
in mental health settings they indicate descending levels of mental development.
Examples of technical terminology include shorthand expressions, or for short,
or a handle for the whole. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:red'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a.
Bases for determining the meaning of words</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Communication
does not work at just the word level. So much ineffective Bible study comes
from dealing with texts at too great a detailwith a verse, a phrase, or even
one word at a time. Readers must determine the meaning of a word carefully. Below
are some inadequate ways of determining word meaningseven though each approach
has a contribution it can make. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> An
example of this somewhat artificial approach is Bill Hallsteds article How
Not to Define Worship (<i>Christian Standard</i>, January 8, 1995, pp. 8-9).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1)
not particularly by derivational connection (word picture)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The present
meaning of a word does not necessarily reflect its derivational origin. Speakers
today may not know where a term came from a hundred years ago. Even if they do
know or can figure out its derivation, they do not use it with that intent. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>**How often have you heard someone
observe that <u>cheerful</u> giver (2 Corinthians <b>9</b>:7) is the word from
which English gets its term <i>hilarious</i>? A hilarious giver does not make sensible
English because of the connotation of the term now.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **Word
derivational has been used to conclude that Phoebe in Romans <b>16</b>:1-2 was
a leader or director of some kind rather than a servant, as the word is most
often translated. The Greek term </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:
"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>(<i>prostatis</i>)</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>combines
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>pro</i>, before) and </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>histmi</i>, stand); hence, the
reasoning goes, a</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'>by word picture is <i>one who stands before</i>,<i> a
leader.</i> But Paul says Phoebe was a </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'> of himself also; she did not lead Paul. Contextually relevant
observations in this case override speculations based on word derivation. She
was a helper as one who <i>stands at hand</i>,<i></i> ready to help.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  <i>Holiday</i>
does not really mean holy day, and <i>business</i> does not particularly mean
busyness. The derivational meaning has altered into an applied meaning.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>We should not expect to learn the meaning of words by where
they came from or what they have descended to.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) not particularly by
combining constituent elements (morphemes) in compound words. In English the
process of combining constituent parts to get the meaning of a word works
better with less-used terms and those derived from Greek and Latin roots.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **<i>Lover</i>
is not simply one who loves. It has become a technical term for an unmarried
physically attracted person. It takes some mental adjustments to sing <i>Jesus,
Lover of My Soul.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3)
not particularly by cognate words within a language</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>**famous, but infamous; in-law but out-law;
offence but offensive; childish, but childlike. Paul does not teach in 1
Corinthians <b>13</b>:11 that speaking in tongues is childish; he compares language
speaking and other gifts to what a child does in contrast to what an adult
does. He means childlike.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  Our
loved ones normally refers to our relatives, even though by fundamental
cognate meaning it could apply much more broadly to anyone people care about.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **<u>Uncouth</u>
does not have a functioning opposite word couth.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  charisma,
but charismatic. In usage, <i>charismatic</i> is not just an adjective for <i>charisma</i>.
It can step away from magnetic personality via enthusiasm and connote
supernatural giftedness in Christian parlance. Other people set <i>charismatic</i>
in contrast to <i>pentecostal</i>, meaning that supernatural gifts are necessary
<i>vs</i>. possible for Christian experience.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  Other
examples include <i>disagree</i> and <i>disagreeable</i>, <i>argumentive</i>
and <i>argumentative</i>, <i>egocentric and egotistical</i>. <i>Excuse</i>, a
verb, has neutral or even good connotation while <i>excuse</i>, a noun, has a
negative one.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  In
1 Timothy <b>2</b>:12</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>authente)</i> indicates something Paul says a woman
should not do relative to men. From the fact that <i>authents</i> (</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>), a cognate noun, at one time had among
its meanings murder, some modern writers have tried to argue that the cognate
verb Paul uses here has a harsh connotation (<i>domineer, usurp</i>)<i> </i>rather
than a neutral one (<i>exercise authority over</i>). The time difference plus
specialization works against using that line of reasoning. No one, including
men, should domineer.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  English
speakers may note that the word picture behind <i>secretary</i> comes from
keeping a secret, but they probably would not think of that unless asked
point blank. People use <i>refer </i>without remembering its historical
roots; they say refer back even though derivationally <i>re-</i> already
means back. Speakers add <i>back</i> to distinguish referring back from
referring forward. (See also <i>What the Bible Says About Salvation</i>, p.
252, 3.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  We
talk about metal filings even when a file did not produce them. <i>Rain check</i>
applies even when rain did not cancel the event or the sale at the grocery
store. Immersionists need to be careful about overly depending on the original
word-picture in </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>baptize)</i>, and instrumentalists should not overly
depend on the derivational meaning of </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:
"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>(<i>psall</i>,
to pluck) to argue for the legitimacy of instrumental music in accompanying
singing. <i>Overnight</i> can come to mean rapid change rather than something
that strictly happened during one nights time. This observation may have a
bearing on how strictly to read Jonah <b>4</b>:10, where a gourd comes up
overnight.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:10.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>ignore and ignorant: <i><u>ignorant</u></i>
does not mean intentionally unaware; yet <i>ignore</i> means deliberately not
paying attention.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  dominant,
but dominate or indomitable; of these three only <i>dominate</i> has a negative
connotation.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>   hard,
but hardly; As the old joke goes, <i>Are you working hard? No. Im hardly
working.</i>) </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  fleshy,
but fleshly </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  submit,
but submissive</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  historical
and historic</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  rest,
but restless: <i>restless</i> does not mean without rest or having less
rest; it indicates peoples condition where they cannot settle down to
anything.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (4)
not particularly by related words across languages</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Scholars
sometimes study rare and one-use words in the Old Testament by comparing them
to Arabic, Akkadian, and other Semitic languages to get some feel for their
meaning, but the method has limitations and should give place to other
approaches whenever possible.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:135.35pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-117.35pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (5) not particularly by
usages elsewhere on other subjects or in other contexts</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The most
frequent usage does not determine meaning in a given case; some statistically
rare meaning may be needed in a specific case. Authors may even coin a
meaning; they may define their own terms for their own purposes in precise,
technical fashion, because they need to speak more precisely than terminology already
available allows them to do. Words are not bullets of meaning we add together
say something. They are more fluid than that, being shaped by the subject
connections in certain settings. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>Spare the rod and spoil the child</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (cp. Proverbs <b>13</b>:24). People
opposed to corporal punishment may bring the shepherds rod and staff into the
discussion, supposing that the rod to <u>protect</u> the sheep by fending off
predators instead of something used on the sheep. Another take is to say that
the rod was used to <u>guide</u> the sheep along. The two circumstances differ
sufficiently that the terms in one case should not define the terms in the
other. The shepherds crook was more for retrieving and guiding than his rod
would be. Shepherding sheep and disciplining children are two unrelated
circumstances; the only thing that connects them is the one word <i>rod</i>!
Hebews <b>12</b>:4-13 shows that the Proverbs passage means something
grievous results from using a rod on a young a son for disobedience.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>New Testament interpreters need to use
caution in understanding Christian vocabulary based on <u>pagan Greek usage</u>.
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>psych, </i>soul),</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>for
example, in <u>pagan Greek</u> probably does not coincide with biblical usage. Pagans
had only experience unassisted by special revelation, hence, perception and reason
alone (philosophical method). We do well to consider more seriously the
likelihood of phenomenological usage of some Greek terms because imagination
and how it looks would be what they could operate from. In each case it is
better to interpret New Testament terms in light of <u>Old Testament</u>
backgrounds and other, <u>clear New Testament</u> usage. New Testament usage came
from the Septuagint translation of the Old Testament more often than from general
Greek backgrounds. The Septuagint enjoyed wide circulation among Jews of the
Diaspora as well as in Palestine. A person would think, for example, that the
Septuagint usage of </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>pistis</i>, faith/trust/belief)</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>in
Habakkuk<b> 2</b>:4 would inform New Testament usage more than pagan Greek
usage.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  In
<u>secular usage</u>, <i>love</i> has become a euphemism for sexual intercourse
or feelings toward another person based on physical attraction.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  The
<u>same word used even in close proximity</u> does not always refer to the same
thing. In Romans <b>7</b>:3 <i>to become to another man</i> must refer to
adultery in the first case but marital relations in the second. 1 Corinthians<b>
7</b>:27 says, <i>Are you bound to a wife? Do not try to be loosed. Are you
loosed from a wife? Do not seek a wife. </i>It is not evident by the second <i>loosed</i>
that Paul means divorced, as interpreters sometime suggest. He probably means
a single person (un-bound rather than dis-bound), which fits better his
preceding comments in<b> 7</b>:8-24. The situation is like Road Closed in
front of a segment that has never yet been open.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (ly)</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>can mean untie or loosen in some
circumstances, but destroy in others.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (6) not particularly by
majority usage</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (7) by general
conventional usage</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (8) most particularly by
the present intent of the author, as shown by the near context, within distant
context, within the nature of the case. Authorial intent provides the real
basis for meaning. The previous seven have secondary and confirmational value. A
word or expression does not mean just anything it could mean</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> Help
yourself does not mean <i>because nobody else will </i>or <i>because youre
big enough</i>; it means You are <u>free</u> to take some. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> <i>We
do it all for you</i> (as the advertisement used to go) does not mean you do
not have to take your food to your table or clean up after yourself. The stress
falls on <i>you</i>, not on <i>all</i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   To some extent,
denotation may be learned from derivation, cognates, and constituent parts. Connotation
especially, though, must come from contextual factors. The meaning of words,
expressions, and sentences lies <u>in themselves</u> (their derivational word
picture plus conventional, contemporary use) and <u>in their relationships</u>
(contextual considerations). Beyond convention, definitions adjust to subject
matter and its contrasts. <u>Usage</u> here determines meaning here.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   In one sense
authorial intent is not absolute. We cannot assume an <u>exact</u> equation
between (1) an authors idea, (2) a readers idea, and (3) reality itself. For
practical purposes we equate the three so we can flesh out what the author <u>does</u>
say into a complete picture, some of which we supply from elsewhere (personal
experience). In so doing, we go beyond authorial intent as far as we can know
it. We give authors the benefit of the doubt by adding other content to
expressed content and then developing, or interpreting, the full picture. We
assume that what authors allow readers to fill in does not have features that
will skew the message.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   Authorial
intent sets aside the <i>avant-garde</i> practice of literary critics who speak
of <i>the intentional fallacy</i> and <i>reader response</i> criticism. Language
is a sound-symbol system. As with all symbols, they mean what the author
intends them to mean, not what some other person, culture, or time could repurpose
them to mean. The Bible is not literature as in modern art and modern
literature, where the <u>artist</u> does not so much say something as provide
a suggestive form for the <u>viewer</u> to create something into it. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> By-passing
authorial intent involves the same fallacy as (a) objecting to <u>the heart</u>
<u>symbol</u> on Valentines Day, because in ancient pagan religions it had
other, immoral meanings. The same issue lies behind (b) objecting to <u>Christmas
trees</u> because evergreens in pagan contexts had fertility associations. We wonder
as well about (c) objecting to jack-o-lanterns and Halloween traditions like
trick or treat. What those things meant to pagans centuries ago need not
condemn people today who are not aware of those origins and certainly do not
mean by them what pagans meant then. The meaning of such things lies in the user
here and now.   Intentionality
is at issue in legal interpretation and constitutional hermeneutics. The claim
is that you cannot rest with original intent because issues have arisen that
the framers of the constitution did not know about. But new circumstances are
the reason legal process calls for <u>amendments</u> and <u>new laws</u>, not
for specious interpretations of older law.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   Authors
intentionality determines <u>where readers look</u>, <u>how they go about
deciding</u>, and <u>what factors they make decisive</u> as qualifiers on other
factors. An author may even coin an expression or use of an old expression in a
new way. In such cases, English writing uses quotation marks to indicate the
special usage. In oral expression, the speaker uses a special voice inflection
to mark the unusual meaning. Ancient writers did not have access to such written
devices.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b.
The multiple meanings of a word </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Most words can mean more
than one thing. Changes over time follow a path of <u>logical</u> <u>connection</u>
between the previous and subsequent meanings; proliferation of meanings follows
associational lines: cause-effect connections, topical associations, similarities
in form or function. Connections with the thing slide over onto a new one. This
amounts to what happens in figures of speech and adaptations over time. Various
connections tie together contemporary meanings of a word. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> The connections are not
always direct and may follow a tree of descent rather than have a common thread.
One element associates one meaning with the next one. The connector between the
first two meanings may not be the connector between the next two meanings, and
so on. The following diagram represents the pattern.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>A physical body (1 Corinthians <b>6</b>:19)
has an associational meaning with Christs body represented by the communion
emblems (1 Corinthians <b>11</b>:29) and by extension his body the church
(Ephesians <b>1</b>:23; 1 Corinthians <b>12</b>:12-31), because it manifests
the presence of Christ in the world after his ascension.</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'><img width=421 height=120
src="/cir-library/images/essays/getting-the-point/image001.png"></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>Although
it is sometimes possible to do so, we should not expect a <u>common core of
meaning</u> in cognates, especially if several applications have evolved. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>Sometimes immersionists have made that
mistake in denying sprinkling and pouring as actions for baptism. The study of
the form of baptism, in fact, provides a relevant example of the relationship
between multiple meanings of one word. (See <i>What the Bible Says About
Salvation</i>, pp. 322-379.) <i>Spirit</i> does seem to have a common core for
most of its meanings: an invisible, intangible reality.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Forgetting about the
multiple meanings of words causes unnecessary difficulty and keeps discussions from
solving anything; they degenerate into unwinnable arguments over words. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>**An example is the argument over
whether Christians will pass through the judgment; the idea is that Christians
will not be judged. The problem arises because the Greek wordeven more than
its English rough equivalentcan mean evaluate or condemn. Evaluation
applies to everyone; condemnation applies to the disobedient.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **The
expression break bread in the New Testament refers both to eating a meal and
observing the Lords Supper. Often the latter evidently took place in the
context of the former in what they called love feasts (Jude 12). Some have
argued that the earliest Christians observed daily communion at home because
Acts <b>2</b>:46 says, <i>The continued daily breaking bread at home.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **The image
of God has a nature and an ethical usageas well as a pattern Christians share
with the Lord in suffering (Romans<b> 8</b>:<u>28</u>-31).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **<i>Hades</i>
is the grave as well as the abode of the dead beyond it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  Multiple
meanings affect Matthew <b>19</b>:12, where Jesus speaks of people who make
themselves eunuchs for the kingdom of heavens sake. Does this mean (1)
self-mutilation (what about women?) or (2) voluntary celibacy (as in 1
Corinthians<b> 7</b>:25-35)? The New International Version assumes the latter
and translates it to mean refrain from marriage.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  3.
Language changes over time.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Reading older translations of
the Bible shows that English words have changed meanings, sometimes drastically,
since those versions were made. Words have fallen out of use; others remain in
use among church people as religious jargon, but they do not communicate well
outside the Christian community. When Christians use these dead words, they
create the impression that the message of Christ itself is a dead, horse-and-buggy
thing. In l955 Thomas Nelson &amp; Sons published a booklet entitled <i>Bible
Words That Have Changed in Meaning</i> (edited by Luther A. Weigle). It listed
hundreds of English words and expressions that have changed meanings or dropped
out of common parlance since the King James Version was made (1611). </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>Perhaps the most extreme change is the word
<i>let</i>, which means prevent in Romans <b>1</b>:13!</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  4.
Language is arbitrary.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <i>The relationship between language
forms and meanings is</i> <i>conventional</i>. Language usage is a matter of
custom, not necessity. Although there are some objective boundaries on application,
no inherent connection exists between sound and referent</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>This observation obtains even in
cases of onomatopoeia: dogs do not really say <i>arf</i>; cats do not say <i>meow</i>;
pigs do not say <i>oink</i>. These are stylized expressions and therefore words.
In other languages the word for a dogs bark or a cats cry is not the same as
it is in English.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  5. Language is characterized by
specialization.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  6.
Meaning is not carried wholly by words.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Meaning is carried in part by
morphemes (meaning units) within a word, by syntactical relationships,
contextual considerations, intonation, and gestures (kinesics). Words are
relatively imprecise, being shaped by accompanying phenomena in the language,
the speaking situation, and the culture. Interpreters have to consider
semantics, syntax, and context. Syntax (grammatical constructions) may not be as
significant as the flow of thought and nearby subject matter (context). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>For an example, see <i><u>Journal of Biblical Literature</u>
</i>(1996), p. 87ff.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  7.
Languages differ as to how they categorize reality.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <i>The relationship between
language forms and the meanings is rarely the same across languages. </i>For
this reason, bogus exegesis happens when people consult an dictionary to get
the meaning of an English word and base presentations about the Bible on the
meanings they find. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>An example appears on page 24 of the
August 21, l988, issue of <i>Christian Standard</i>: The Lords Name in Vain,
by Donnie Smith. Many true things get said this way, but not because of the
basis given.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Different languages organize
reality differently. While reality itself has objective categories, speakers
impress additional categories on it. They supply logical and psychological
distinctions onto the reality much as geographers project administrative
districts like countries, states, counties, and townships onto a topographical
map.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Not categorizing reality the
same way is partly a function of vocabulary available to a speakers own language.
In January, 1995, the <i>Readers Digest </i>reported that English has an
active vocabulary of some 650,000 words, three times that of any other language.
A core vocabulary of 50,000 words ranks high with even modern European
languages, whose active vocabularies range around 25,000. This abundance of
terminology eclipses the New Testament vocabulary of 5,436 items, 3,246 of
which occur three times or fewer (Metzger, <i>Lexical Aids</i>, p. 1). The
Hebrew-Aramaic vocabulary in the Old Testament is even more limited. Individual
words in biblical languages must be more general and versatile than English
ones, and the reader must depend more on context and the nature of the case. This
disparity in vocabulary is probably one reason modern Western interpreters appeal
too much to terms when hearing other languages and in reading the Bible.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>**The biblical word <i>give/gift</i>
does not have as broad a range in English as does the corresponding Hebrew word
</span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
dir=LTR></span><span lang=HE style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'><span
dir=LTR></span> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>(<i>natan</i>)
or the Greek word </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>didmi</i>). English dos not as
naturally allow for indirect operation (however, cp. gifted as in talented; who
gives this woman to be married to this man? said in a society where marriage
does not eliminate the free will of a woman in choosing a spouse). 1
Corinthians <b>15</b>:38 says, <i>God gives [the kernel] a body even as it
pleased him, and to each seed a body of its own.</i> Such an expression does
not conflict with our understanding of genetics. <i>Give</i> can also cover allow,
especially in Hebrew. 2 Kings <b>5</b>:1 says that Yahveh gave victory to
Syria/Aram, yet Naaman, as well as his master, was a worshiper of Rimmon (<b>5</b>:18-19).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>gar</i>),</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>usually translated for, does not
always mean for or because in a sequential sense. (Mark <b>5</b>:42: <i>And
immediately the girl rose up and walked, for she was twelve years old.</i>) So
Bible readers should not overdo the old dictum, When you see a therefore,
see what it is there for. Often </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;
color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> is illative; that is, it broadens (<i>latus</i>)
and continues the thought by adding something associated with previous
information. It expands on the previous point. <i>Wherefore/therefore</i> can
mean (1) it derives from the previous point or (2) it is compatible with the
previous point.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **Hebrew has no separate
form for past perfect (had done); so Genesis <b>2</b> could be translated
God created or God had created (a feature of Hebrew that fiat creationists
might use to remove the conflict between the creation sequence in Genesis <b>1</b>
and Genesis <b>2</b>).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **<i>Fear</i> in
English has a more narrow range of meaning than the Greek </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>phobe</i>), which can also cover respect.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **Hebrew uses <i>heal</i>
to cover a greater variety of corrective processes than the English word does. It
can fix a political situation, the climate, something physical, psychological,
or spiritual.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **Hebrew <i>mashal</i>
ranges more widely than the English <i>rule</i>. That man would rule over
woman does not have to imply negativity. It is the same word that describes a
shepherd who protects and provides for his sheep.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **<i>One another</i>
in English tends to be two-directional/reciprocal; in Greek the corresponding
term </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;
color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif;color:#7030A0'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'>(<i>allln</i>) may also be one-directional in a groupone to
another. That affects the interpretation of Ephesians <b>5</b>:21; James <b>5</b>:16,
and other texts.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>(<i>akou</i>) is broader
than (a) <i>hear</i>. It covers hearing with the ear, (b) listening, as in
paying attention, and (c) heeding, as in taking something to heart. Hearers
may know someone is talking, but they are not registering; they may hear the
words but not hearken to the warning.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **<i>Save</i> covers
more than spiritual salvation; it refers also to relief from slavery,
execution, physical illness, and so on.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **The same can
be said for <i>hate</i>, <i>be silent</i>, and <i>submit</i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  8.
Languages differ in the conventional usages of even the elements they share.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Scripture was not written in our
mother tongue; so we are hindered by working only from an English Bible. About
all we can do is compare translations, read commentaries, and use language
helps that give some access to the Hebrew, Aramaic, and Greek we do not know. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Two languages may both have the
same verbal meanings availableaspect, tense, voice, number, person, gender;
they may share certain syntactical features and vocabulary items. However, they
may not use the same <u>idiom</u>. Idiom is the <u>way</u> a language uses its
mechanics as distinguished from the available mechanics themselves. Two
languages may not use their same mechanics at the same point. Languages differ
in how much they conventionally expect the listener to infer from the nature of
the case.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>**Greek is satisfied to use the article in places where
English calls for the possessive adjective. For example, the gospel account may
say, <i>Jesus laid <u>the</u> hands</i> [or, laid hands] <i>on the sick man</i>;
but English wants to specify <u>whose</u> hands even though context makes that
sufficiently clear: <i>Jesus laid <u>his</u> hands on the sick man.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **Greek is
satisfied with using simple past for actions previous to other past actions. Mark
<b>3</b>:10, for example, says, <i>He healed many</i>; English wants to say, <i>He
had healed many.</i> Greek depends on the reader to infer the sequence from what
went on. English wants to make explicit that obvious sequence, so it uses the
past perfect where Greek does not bother to use its pluperfect.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **Greek articles
are used differently from English ones. English has nouns with no article, with
definite article, and with indefinite article (in the singular). Greek has
article and no article. The pie is being cut up in a different way, and it is
not really the same pie. There is no easy rule for correlating articular usage
in the two languages, as when people try to say <i>the</i> in English each time
Greek has its article and <i>a</i> in English when Greek has no article. Early
on, someone misunderstood this point and tried to create an argument against
the ontological deity of Jesus by putting <i>a </i>in front of <i>God</i> in
John <b>1</b>:1ff. John means the word was deity. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> Others have
translated Luke <b>18</b>:8, <i>When the Son of man comes, will he find <u>the</u>
faith on the earth? </i>[in contrast to a future perversion of Christian
teaching]. Jesus point is more general, asking whether he will find faith on
the earth. The Greek article means identity, and sets the noun over against
parallel alternatives. Its absence means quality and draws attention to the
characteristics of the referent; hearers are not to let their mind soft focus
on the object. The distinction is sometimes awkward to bring into English
idiom. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>  **Idiomatic differences
between Hebrew, Greek, and English on articles, prompt us to ask whether <i>Judge
nothing before <u>the</u> time</i> (1 Corinthians <b>4</b>:5) means simply, <i>Dont
jump to conclusions</i> rather than, <i>Dont judge anything before the
judgment day. </i>The presence of <i>the</i> probably throws us off. The
command may, in fact, be more general.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:10.0pt'>  </span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>**The range of meanings in </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'> (<i>mise</i>)</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>is broader than the word <i>hate</i>
that is<i> </i>usually used to render it. English uses <i>hate </i>as a
negative for <i>love</i>, but Greekat least under the influence of Hebrew
idiomcontrasts love with both negative and neutral attitudes, especially in
statements of contrast. Romans<b> 9</b>:13, for example, quotes Gods statement
in Malachi <b>1</b>:2-3, <i>I loved Jacob and hated Esau;</i> that is, <i>I
set Esau aside.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> Matthew <b>10</b>:37
(= Luke <b>14</b>:26) records Jesus as saying, <i>The one <u>loving</u> father
and mother </i>[and so on] <i><u>more than</u> me is not worthy of me.</i> But
in a parallel passage Luke <b>14</b>:26 puts it, <i>If people come to me and
do not <u>hate</u> their own father</i> [<i>etc</i>.]<i>yes, even their own
life as well, they cannot be a disciple of mine.</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> John<b> 12</b>:25
combines with Luke <b>14</b>:26 in speaking about hating ones own life, yet
