Paul says, <i>People never hated their own flesh</i> (Ephesians<b> 5</b>:29).
Genesis <b>29</b>:31, 33 says Jacob loved Rachel and hated Leah (cp.
Deuteronomy <b>21</b>:15-17); that is, <i>He loved Rachel more than Leah.</i>
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> In the Sermon on
the Mount, Jesus teaches, <i>People cannot serve two masters; for either they
will hate the one and love the other, or they will hold to the one and despise
the other; you cannot serve God and mammon</i> (Matthew <b>6</b>:24). The word
<i>despise</i> here is also used in Hebrews <b>12</b>:2, <i>. . . who for the
joy that was set before him endured the cross, despising the shame.</i> Hating
one master and loving the other compares with holding to one and despising the
other and parallels God and riches, which are not antithetical except as
ultimate frames of referencethe point of the slavery image. Loving one more
than the other seems satisfactory here, whereas in Hebrews <b>12</b>:2 <i>despise</i>
has the feel of disregard. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> The rabbinic
gloss on <i>Love your neighbor</i> [and hate your enemy]<i></i> (Matthew <b>5</b>:43)
may also have been a positive-neutral contrast rather than a positive-negative
one. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> As a result, <i>hate
</i>has a range of usages all the way from hate as in English to disregard,
to love less than. It can represent <u>exaggeration</u>, <u>relative negative</u>,
but not <u>literal</u>. The neutral-negative variable for love and hate also
comes into the discussion of John<b> 3</b>:16-21; <b>4</b>:39-42; Romans<b> 9</b>:13.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> A similar
phenomenon occurs when Samuel selects David to replace Saul. When Jesse brought
his son Eliab, the Lord told the prophet that he had <i>rejected</i> him (1
Samuel<b> 8</b>:6); but when Jesse brought Abinadab forward, the Lord said, <i>I
have not chosen him</i> <b>(8</b>:8, 9).</span><span dir=RTL></span><span
dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'><span dir=RTL></span> </span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>Reject</span></i><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> evidently approximates <i>not choose.</i> In this
case, positive and neutral are in language terms treated as positive and
negative. Rejecting amounts only to passing over in some cases. The love-hate,
reject-take variations are part of a general Hebrew characteristic of dealing
with matters in binary fashion when they can actually be negative, neutral, or
positive. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Note the connection with Law
of Contrast below.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  9.
The flexibility of language</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <b>One big plague in biblical
interpretation is not allowing language to have its naturally flexible
character. </b>(See Hindrances to Interpretation: Technicalizing Terminology.)
Its flexibility shows up in a number of the characteristics already noted: terms
not always meaning the same thing, terms changing over time, using terms in
loose ways, coining new usages, and the rest. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  People interpret written
language more closely than spoken language, possibly because they suppose that
writers choose their words more carefully. Speakers do not have as much time to
choose the wording of their ideas, they can add another statement for
clarification, and they can rely on other tools like gestures, stress, and
voice inflection. Since readers expect writers to be more deliberate, they read
more rigidly. They expect the Bible to have the precision of a book on science
or philosophy, but it is written in the vernacular for common people to deal
with practical problems. Its fluidity is due additionally to being written by
more than forty authors over a period of 1500 years. Exact expression and
consistent usage do not obtain under such conditions.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  One difference a word may
develop is connotation <i>vs</i>. denotation <i>vs</i>. word picture. <u>Word
picture</u> is the image a word like <i>automobile</i> derived from (self-moving).
<u>Denotation</u> is quantitative reference; <u>connotation</u> is qualitative
reference like positive-negative attitudes and shallow-deep degree (intensity).
Denotation addresses the factitive, the objective, the discrete; connotation
addresses degree matters. Denotation is what a word normally refers to (car <i>vs</i>.
bus, tractor, bicycle, motorcycle, airplane). Connotation adds affective
associations picked up through custom of usage or the subject it is typically
attached to. Connotation includes good <i>vs.</i> bad, personal <i>vs</i>.
impersonalespecially in English; intensity and degree. In sum, word picture helps
least in getting the point.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>The word</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>Jews</span></i><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> regularly has a negative connotation in John, referring
to the Jews that rejected Jesus as Messiah. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> Tradition</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> has a good connotation in 1 Corinthians
<b>11</b>:2; 2 Thessalonians <b>2</b>:15, <b>3</b>:6; but a negative one in
Galatians <b>1</b>:14 and Colossians<b> 2</b>:8.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  10. The ambiguity of language (See </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>Inscripturation:
Sources of Ambiguity as well as Confusing Language and Reality.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  C. Nature of Communication</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Communication situations have five
aspects. The <u>referent</u> is the person, place, thing, or abstraction
referred to. <u>Perception</u> is the appropriation of that object into the
mind. <u>Conception</u> is the idea formed in the mind. The <u>expression</u>
encodes in language the speakers conception. The <u>decoded idea</u> is what
hearers conjure up in their own mind when they hear the speakers expression. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <img width=386 height=134
src="/cir-library/images/essays/getting-the-point/image002.png"></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Perversions can occur at any
junction between these aspects. In biblical communication, inspiration
guarantees against error up through the encoding, but error can come in the
illumination aspect because no promise guarantees against error in decoding. That
is the reason for studying hermeneutics. We must learn how to think, how
language operates, how the biblical languages operate, how our own language
operates, how to transition from the former to the latter and from that culture
to ours, how to generalize, and how to apply.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>I. THE BIBLE: THE DIVINE AND THE HUMAN</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  A. Relationship of divine and human in writing
the Bible (inscripturation)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  1. Distinction
between theological and philosophical method</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Theology uses reason on
revelation in terms of experience to understand content; philosophy uses reason
on experience to generate content. Theologyat least Christian theologyis
interpersonal; most philosophies are impersonal in their ultimate frames of
reference.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <img width=331 height=150
src="/cir-library/images/essays/getting-the-point/image003.png"></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  2.
Range of possible human-divine interaction (See Five Models for Completely
Reliable Scripture.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a. Dictation of <u>words</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b. Implantation of <u>ideas</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c. Given <u>abilities</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  d. Granted <u>experiences</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  e. Bestowed <u>offices</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  3. Sources
of ambiguity in scripture </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Two kinds of ambiguity in
language communication are (1) equivocation and (2) amphibole. <u>Equivocation</u>
means using in an unclear way a word that has multiple meanings</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>: He was lying there stoned on the
beach (drunk or pelted with rocks?).</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> <u>Amphibole</u>
means putting syntactical elements together in unclear ways</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>: <i>. . . Mary and Joseph and the babe
lying in the manger </i>(all three or just the baby in the manger?).</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Equivocation is ambiguity between word and referent;
amphibole is ambiguity between one word and another word. Problems can occur on
the delivery side of communication, which hopefully occur in scripture only in
places not materially affecting faith and practice. The sources of ambiguity
below lie more in the nature of situations than in the quality of language
expression itself.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a.
Language deficiencies </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The imprecision of
language combines with the fact noted before that it does not reproduce the situation,
but only enough to call up the full picture in a listeners mind as known from
elsewhere. To some extent, meaning is private to authors because they do not
speak their whole mind. It operates like a code by which a document may be
called up on a computer, a symbol that stands for a larger amount of
information than it specifies. <b>(a)</b> In most cases, a hearer already knows
much or even all the information. A speaker needs only to draw attention to it.
Furthermore, <b>(b) </b>hearers can figure out some things on their own. In
fact, such active listening makes listening more interesting. If speakers say
everything, they risk talking it to deathsomething we do to get a sense of
psychological control over what is bothering us or because we fear that others
are looking for omissions to fill in, loopholes to top, or excuses to correct.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The same thing happens
in Bible reading that happens in everyday conversation. Readers can foist off their
own interests on a writers statements and thereby supply a context that shapes
what they hear. A distinction in their mind may not be in the speakers mind
and <i>vice versa</i>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Language is most
deficient in <b>(a) </b><u>abstract</u> <i>vs</i>. concrete references and in <b>(b)</b>
the <u>affective</u><b> </b><i>vs</i>. cognitive (subjective <i>vs</i>.
objective) ones. These items are not so easy for other people to flesh out from
their own experience or to convey from speakers experience to hearers
awareness.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>**Mary, the mother of Jesus (Luke <b>1</b>:18),
and Zechariah, the father of John the Baptist (Luke <b>1</b>:34), react similarly
to the divine messenger about the future birth of their sons, but the two
responses evidently differed in a way that the language does not reflect.
Zechariah was struck dumb; Mary was not.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Greek loves <u>participles</u>,
which leave unclear the distinctions within the verbal idea: time <i>vs</i>.
means, manner, cause, condition, concession, purpose, result, coordinate
circumstance, and attendant circumstance. The most accurate translation uses
English participles for Greek ones, because that makes the English as ambiguous
as the original. It puts the English reader at the same disadvantage an
original reader would have experienced.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Greekespecially under
Hebrew influenceputs one noun in modifying relationship with another by using the
<u>genitive case</u>. The genitive case merely connects the one noun to the
other; <u>how</u> their referents connect remains unspecified: the genitive
noun may cover for description and possession as well as objective, subjective,
appositive, or originating relationship</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'>. </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>A
reader</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>has to figure out which manner of
connection applies to each case. Again, the most accurate English rendering
uses a ___ of ___ format.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> <i>Coals
of fire</i> (Romans <b>12</b>:20) means fiery/burning coals; <i>man of God</i>
means godly man but could also mean spokesman for God. <i>To the praise of
the glory of the grace of him</i> (Ephesians<b> 1</b>:6) means to the praise
of his glorious grace. Romans <b>8</b>:35, 39 speak of the love of God; but is
it Gods love for us or our love for God? In this case <b>8</b>:37 apparently
resolves the difficulty.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  For examples of the other
kinds of ambiguity in biblical languages, see Selected Linguistic Phenomena.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b.
Written language deficiencies</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Writing a language loses
several cues to meaning: facial expressions, gestures (kinesics), voice
inflection for emphasis, pauses, which particularly affect the connotation of
statementssarcasm, hyperbole, humor</span><span style='font-size:10.0pt'>. </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Plato preferred oral
over written presentation because the oral carried the meaning better. Papias may
have meant the same thing in preferring oral testimony of those who had known
the apostles. Besides, in oral communication the hearer can ask questions. If
these were his reasons, it is not proper to use Papias comment to prioritize
tradition over scripture.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The loss of these
features in written language expression affords significant meaning loss. Researchers
believe that <u>non-verbal cues</u> transmit more than half the meaning; <u>manner
of speaking</u> supplies about a third of the meaning; <u>words</u> by
themselves convey only 10%! The implication for ambiguity in written revelation
is obvious.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (1)
<u>No punctuation marks </u>appear in the earliest Greek manuscripts. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (a) Quotation
marks: Where do Jesus words end in John <b>3</b>? Where do Pauls comments to
Peter end in the Galatians<b> 2</b> account of the Antioch interchange (at
verse 14?)? Perhaps in these cases the lack of a quotation-mark convention
allowed the writers to fade out of quoted into unquoted material without a
concern to mark an inconsequential change.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (b) Question
marks: although some questions can be recognized as questions on the basis of
vocabulary (interrogative words, a distinctive negative for questions
anticipating a negative response), in several cases, New Testament sentences could
be statements or questions: <b>Matthew</b> <b>16</b>:2-3; <b>22</b>:17a; <b>23</b>:37;
<b>24</b>:3; <b>25</b>:26; <b>26</b>:45, 50, 55, 62a, 64a; <b>27</b>:11b, 42; <b>Mark</b>
<b>2</b>:16; <b>4</b>:21b?, 28; (<b>7</b>:18-19); <b>9</b>:11, 28; <b>13</b>:4;
<b>14</b>:41, 48, 64a, 68; <b>15</b>:2c, 31; <b>Luke</b> <b>4</b>:34, 36; <b>11</b>:13;
<b>12</b>:49-50; <b>13</b>:34; <b>17</b>:18; <b>19</b>:22; <b>20</b>:2, 52, 70;<b>
23</b>:3; <b>John</b> <b>4</b>:48; <b>7</b>:28; <b>8</b>:25;<b> 12</b>:19, 27; <b>14</b>:2;
<b>16</b>:32; <b>18</b>:37; <b>20</b>:29; <b>Acts</b> <b>2</b>:11?;<b> 5</b>:4?,
8?; <b>9</b>:21b?; <b>10</b>:29?;<b> 23</b>:9?; <b>Romans</b> <b>2</b>:23; <b>8</b>:33,
34;<b> 11</b>:2-3, 12, 24; <b>1 Corinthians 1</b>:13a; <b>5</b>:2, 12b, 13a; <b>6</b>:4;
<b>12</b>:15, <b>16</b>; <b>2 Corinthians</b> <b>10</b>:7; 1<b>1</b>:8; <b>12</b>:19;
<b>Galatians 4</b>:10; <b>Hebrews</b> <b>9</b>:14, 17; <b>10</b>:2, 29; <b>James</b>
<b>2</b>:1, 18, 19, 22; <b>4</b>:5  quotation, 15?; <b>5</b>:6b; <b>2 Peter 3</b>:11-12.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (c) Commas:
without these marks the Greek text does not differentiate restrictive and
non-restrictive clauses. That distinction must be inferred from context and
topic.</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>Editors supply punctuation in modern
texts, and the rules do not match current English usage. In fact, the
punctuation rules in King James English up into the twentieth century differed
from those used today, especially as regards restrictive and non-restrictive
clauses, Old English set off all clauses with commas regardless.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>(d) no periods: where a sentence ends
is unclear in a few cases.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>John<b> 1</b>:3-4 presents just such a
variance: <i>Without him not even one thing came to be that has come to be. In
him was life and . . . Without him not even one thing came to be. What has
come to be in him was life and . . . </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (2)
The Hebrew texts of the Old Testament originally had <u>no vowel points</u></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>, which causes ambiguity in cases like
Genesis <b>1</b>:1 and Psalm <b>22</b>:16 (cp. John <b>19</b>:36); Hebrews <b>1</b>:8
&lt; Psalm <b>45</b>:6.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (3)
The New and Old Testament provide <u>abbreviated accounts</u>, which are
amazingly concise for the range of subject matter and the time span they cover</span><span
style='font-size:10.0pt'>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:10.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>The conversion accounts in Acts do not
always mention every aspect of the converts response. Repentance is mentioned
in one, belief in another, baptism is not always mentioned. The speeches in
Acts as well as the discourses and sayings material in the gospels surely
summarize more expanded presentations.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:135.0pt;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-117.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (4) The earliest New
Testament manuscripts had <u>no word divisions</u>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (5) New Testament
writers use of <u>amanuenses</u> might introduce stylistic differences that
affect critical introduction issues like authorship.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (6)
Some passages may intend <u>sarcasm</u>, but they contain no written indicators
to that effect. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'><i>They said to him, Sir, evermore
give us this bread</i> (John </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> <b>6</b>:35).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> A
reader could miss the light sarcasm in Pauls comment to the Corinthians<i>,
Forgive me this wrong!</i> (2 Corinthians <b>12</b>:13). It seems unlikely
that he was admitting error in not taking financial support since he states his
intention to continue the practice (<b>12</b>:14).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> Some
have suggested that <i>Christian</i> had a derogatory connotation at first, but
we cannot determine that from Acts <b>11</b>:26: <i>They were called
Christians first in Antioch. </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>(7)</span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt'> Some cases may have conveyed <u>humor</u></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> In
Luke<b> 14</b>:20 one guest invited to a feast said he could not come because
he had got married. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> Jesus
speaks of a log being in someones eye (Matthew <b>7</b>:3-5).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (8) Unlike modern
English that can use underlining, italics, bold print, quotation marks, or even
all capitals, the Bible has <u>limited devices for indicating stress</u>. As a
result, writers could not show which word in a sentence should be stressed. For
examples of the difference it makes to stress various words in a sentence, see
Misreading Restrictive and Non-Restrictive. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Greek can
indicate emphasis with an express pronoun subject. Since verb endings indicate
person and number, adding a nominative-case pronoun normally adds stress to the
subject. This may not always hold true, however, especially in Johns writings.
Some nominative pronouns do not seem to fit with a need for emphasis. Instead,
he may be reflecting a Semitic tendency for subject pronouns without
accompanying linking verbs to cover the is of identification. Second
attributive adjectives may imply more stress than first attributive ones, but
that observation probably does not hold true in Revelation, which has so many
of them. Reversing the normal order in a Greek sentence tends to emphasize a
word that is, say, put at the head or end of the sentence.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (9)
Greek had <u>no lower-higher-case variable</u> in the original uncial Greek manuscripts;
they were all in capitals (without punctuation or word divisions). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> Several
passages leave uncertain whether to capitalize the word <i>S/spirit</i> as in
Galatians<b> 5</b>:16-26 and elsewhere. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> In
Philippians <b>4</b>:2 commentators have proposed that true yokefellow
should be rendered as a proper name: true <i><u>Syzygos</u></i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (10)
Manuscripts have <u>no device for hyphenation</u>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>Ephesians <b>4</b>:11 is probably not pastors
and teachers, but pastor-</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> teachers.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> Modern
English affords some cases where written form is clearer than oral. (a) The
proliferation of <u>homophones</u> in English creates situations where written
is clearer because the spelling is different. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>In Hark! the Herald Angels Sing the
third verse says, Hail the Sun of righteousness. A listener cannot tell <i>sun</i>
from <i>son</i>. Greek and Hebrew have hardly any homophones whereas English is
loaded with them. About the closest thing comes the textual variant between </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'>(<i>chrstos</i>, good) and </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> (<i>Christos</i>, Christ) in 1 Peter <b>2</b>:3. It is
either a pun or an iticism (an </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> to </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'></span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> shift).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>(b) English <u>punctuation</u>
sometimes clarifies the difference between restrictive and non-restrictive
modifying clauses and phrases. That distinction must be inferred from context
in the New Testament, because the earliest Greek manuscripts had no punctuation.  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Written language has the
advantage of <u>permanence</u>; writing can better retain peoples statements
after they are gone (2 Peter <b>1</b>:12-15;<b> 3</b>:1-2). It makes <u>multiplication</u>
easier and allows for a more thoughtful wording of ideas, which enhances <u>clarity</u>
and <u>efficiency</u>. A final advantage is <u>accuracy;</u> written materials prevent
fewer errors from creeping in than do memory and oral tradition.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c.
Textual-transmission errors</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Not much ambiguity has
been created over the centuries by miscopying</span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;color:#7030A0'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>scripture</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>.</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> Most
typos are readily recognizable as such. On a few occasions, matters of minor
consideration arise from textual divergence. The major New Testament textual variants
appear in the conclusion to the Lords Prayer (Matthew<b> 6</b>:13b), the
conclusion to Marks gospel (<b>16</b>:9-20), Luke <b>9</b>:55b (<i>You do not
know what kind of spirit you are of</i>); the woman taken in adultery (John <b>7</b>:53-<b>8</b>:11),
Acts <b>8</b>:37 (<i>If you believe, you may.</i>), Acts <b>15</b>:34 (<i>But
it seemed good to Silas to remain there</i>), misplacements of the benediction
and doxology in Romans <b>16</b>:24 + 25-27, the troubling of the water in John
<b>5</b>:3b-4, the three witnesses passage in 1 John <b>5</b>:8. Textual
variants do not affect any doctrinal issues. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  d.
Translation difficulties</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Since Bible study works
across language barriers, it loses some precision; modern languages and
biblical languages do not correlate fully. The problem is compounded by time lapse
and cultural drift across twenty-one centuries. In a few cases, events
originally recorded in Aramaic and discussions originally carried on in Aramaic
have come down in a Greek record translated into English by scholars with
various doctrinal biases and linguistic skills.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The Greek of the New
Testament has been somewhat influenced by Hebrew idiom since all the writers
were Jews. So, good translation and proper exegesis must take into account the influence
of Hebrew idiom on Greek usage.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  As with all languages,
Greek during the first century was in flux. It was, for example, losing the
original significance of the <u>middle voice</u>. Romans<b> 9</b>:22,
accordingly, could be translated vessels fitted for destruction, vessels
that fitted themselves for destruction, or, more likely, vessels fit for
destruction. Some matters of <u>vocabulary</u> are not always clear: because/that
for </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (<i>hoti)</i>;
for/in/among/in the manner of/by for </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;font-family:"Cambria Math",serif'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (<i>en)</i> +
dative case (note baptize in/with water). <i>Is</i> is often not expressed:
Ephesians <b>6</b>:17; 2 Timothy<b> 3</b>:16; Hebrews <b>1</b>:8. Vocative and
nominative are alike in most cases; so Hebrews <b>1</b>:8 says, <i>Your throne,
O God, (is) forever and ever </i>or, less likely, <i>Your throne (is) God
forever and ever.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  e. Cross-cultural
difference</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The cultural disjunction
between biblical times and the present Western world leads to reading the New
and Old Testaments without the proper framework in mind. Furthermore, it is
unclear at times whether the original writers presented within their own implicit
cultural limitation or whether they were speaking transculturally for all time.
This difficulty relates to the need to distinguish advice from commandment,
certain marriage issues, and several social questions. 1 Corinthians<b> 7</b>:36-38
probably relates to a fathers giving his daughter in marriage rather than a
fianc deciding to marry his girlfriend. What was the significance of
veil-wearing, and what was the consequent custom of usage? Table fellowship among
Jews implied more than eating together would today in the Western World (Acts [<b>10</b>:9-16]
<b>10</b>:28; <b>11</b>:2-3; Galatians<b> 2</b>:11ff.). Shunning may not
pack as much punch in modern Western societies (Romans <b>16</b>:17; 2
Corinthians <b>5</b>:9-13; 2 Thessalonians<b> 3</b>:6, 14; Titus <b>3</b>:10-11;
2 John 10)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  f.
Time factor</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Because modern
interpreters are so long removed from biblical events, they have difficulty
recovering information about customs, places, word usage, and the like. Some
supporting documents no longer exist. Archaeology may help somewhat to
alleviate this difficulty.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  g.
Interpretation problems</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Even when speakers
address each other in the same culture, time, and language, they do not always hear
each other right because of pride, presupposition, irrationalism, and insufficient
concept inventory. (See Hindrances to interpretation.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  A couple factors help compensate
for these sources of ambiguity: (a) understanding the unclear in light of the
clear (Ephesians <b>5</b>:21 in light of Colossians<b> 3</b>:18; Titus <b>3</b>:5;
1 Peter <b>3</b>:1, for example) and (b) understanding the situational in light
of the general.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  B. Resultant nature of scripture (bibliology)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  1. Is, contains, becomes the Word of
God</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a.
The Bible <u>is</u> the word of God.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  When evangelicals use
this statement, they do not mean to disregard the distinction between medium and
message, words and referent. They subscribe to the concept Beyond the sacred
page I see thee, Lord. By <i>is</i> they mean correlates with the truth as
reality itself; they mean it is as if God himself isd speaking there In
doctrinal discussions and scholarly endeavors, interpreters can get lost in
intellectual reasoning at the word level, or they do not profit from the
message because they seek understanding without seeking God.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b.
The Bible <u>contains</u> the word of God.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <i>Contains the word of
God</i> implies that the Bible is not all Gods word. Mixed in with spiritual insight
are the words of people, including wicked laws and common misunderstandings of
the day in scientific, historical, and geographical matters. This viewpoint has
been associated especially with the older modernism with its historical
skepticism and low regard for the accuracy of biblical materials. It is
invokedperhaps unconsciouslyby Christians that do not feel comfortable being
at odds with modern notions about general evolution, corporal punishment, and
homosexual behavior. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c.
The Bible <u>becomes</u> the word of God.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Karl Barth popularized
the idea that scripture becomes the word of God in contrast to contains it
or is it. There are senses in which the phrase becomes the word of God could
carry a helpful idea. If the written communication is distinguished from the
referent, then (1) when people read the text, <u>they become aware of the
referent</u>. In that process it could be said that the word becomes for them the
word of God. That concept, however, blurs inspiration and illumination because
it melts together objective meaning and subjective appropriation of that
meaning as well as behavioral, interpersonal relationship to him of whom it
speaks. In a way, objective and subjective do blend together in interpersonal
encounter; but content that establishes values for behavior, makes promises
about the future, preserves accounts of Gods mighty acts, and gives
descriptive teaching about God and creation is not susceptible to becoming. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Becoming the word of
God in such cases approaches the concept of (2) <u>eschatological method</u>. This
method of theologizing says the meaning of an event is what that event leads
towhat it becomes. The meaning of the Christ event lies in its impact on
subsequent human history. Impression (viewer) substitutes for intention
(Author). Christ and what he did became meaningful because of differences it
kicked up afterward; <u>what</u> he meant lies in the legacy that descended
from him. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> However, scripture offers
more than a series of events that by hindsight lead to their end products. It
is not a matter of seeing the end and letting it select out of the random
events that culminate in an eventuation point. It is not that the end selects
the events that produce it; God declares the end from the beginning. So the
meaning of the events is <u>in</u> the events rather than <u>read into</u> them
by perceivers who are affected by them and react so as to bring about the events
inspired by this Person. Therefore, scripture does not record the reflections
of perceivers, but records revelation from God about the intended significance
of those events. Interpreting that revelation means striving to apprehend meaning
injected by God rather than meaning perceived by men.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;color:#7030A0'>This scenario can be illustrated by the
life of Joseph. As a young man he dreamed about the moon and stars bowing to
him. Any young boy might have a dream like his; but in Josephs case his
dominance over his brothers actually came to pass later and in striking
fashion. When it came to pass, the original dream became the word of God. Other
boys dreams did not materialize, so their dreams were not revelation that
could become the word of God.</span><span style='font-size:10.0pt'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Information and events have the character of
revelation because it is as if God is speaking, who always speaks truly.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Containing and becoming
the word of God fall short of the claim scripture makes for itself. In the
proper approach, the interpreter grants the claimas provisionally guaranteed
by the omnipotence and omniscience of God (theoretical approach)and measures
the features of biblical content against that claim (phenomenological
approach).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  2.
Inerrancy, infallibility, sufficiency, authority</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  See notations in
God-Breathedness as the Character of Scripture. The inerrancy issue has
importance primarily for <u>interpretation</u> more than to <u>apologetics</u>.
It is a question of <u>meaning</u> more than <u>truth</u>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  3.
The Bible and other books</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a. Strictly speaking, the
Bible as a book written in human language is interpreted like any other example
of human language. The issue is not who speaks through this medium, but the
nature of the medium and the rules that govern its use.    We
are talking here about how language operates, not about what it says. It is a
consideration of form, not contentlinguistics, not meaning. The approach does
not imply that since other books make false claims, we can deny, say, that supernatural
things in scripture are literally true because such things would be false
elsewhere. An interpreter attempts to get at what the author <u>meant</u>
rather than decide whether it is true; interpreting and believing are two
different endeavors. The idea, for example, is not to justify the anti-supernatural
bias in modern Western societies, but to understand the Bible. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:6.0pt;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> Much
discussion today on womens issues, for example, slides around on the evidence
because people will not let New Testament writers say what they mean. Deciding
on application in current situations raises a different question whose answer
depends on unvoiced assumptions Paul was making. The application step requires
finding legitimate ways to discover those assumptionsas based on the nature of
the issue, the kinds of reasons he gave, the ramifications of supposing an
implicit cultural limitation. We must let Paul and other writers say what <u>they</u>
want to say on such topics; then we decide whether it is true or whether we can
make adjustments in applying it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b. Readers allow for
inconsistency in normal books and understand the conflicting parts as they seem
individually to be meant. In the Bible, however, interpreters keep working with
it till they figure it out harmoniously; they do so because they begin with the
assumption that the Bible harmonizes throughout. They do so because of the
claim it makes for itself, and they measure the phenomena against the claim. When
we assume speakers are telling the truth, we give them the benefit of the
doubt, as the saying goes. That means an authors words are taken in a way
that fits with viewpoints known from elsewhere, not in a way that conflicts
with reality or what the author says elsewhere.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> 
</span><span style='font-size:11.0pt'>christir.org</span></p>
</div>
